                                    <div class="article-header">
    <p class="category text-uppercase">ESET Research</p>
    <h1 class="page-headline">MoustachedBouncer: Espionage against foreign diplomats in Belarus</h1>    <p class="sub-title">Long-term espionage against diplomats, leveraging email-based C&C protocols, C++ modular backdoors, and adversary-in-the-middle (AitM) attacks… Sounds like the infamous Turla? Think again!</p>
    
    <div class="article-authors d-flex flex-wrap"><div class="article-author d-flex"><a href="/en/our-experts/matthieu-faou/" title="Matthieu Faou"><picture><source srcset="https://web-assets.esetstatic.com/tn/-x45/wls/mfaou.jpeg" media="(max-width: 768px)" /><img class="author-image me-3"  src="https://web-assets.esetstatic.com/tn/-x45/wls/mfaou.jpeg" alt="Matthieu Faou"  /></picture></a><div class="author-text"><p><a href="/en/our-experts/matthieu-faou/" title="Matthieu Faou"><b>Matthieu Faou</b></a></p></div></div></div>
    <p class="article-info mb-5">
        <span>10 Aug 2023</span>
        <span class="d-none d-lg-inline">&nbsp;&bull;&nbsp;</span>
        <span class="d-inline d-lg-none">, </span>
        <span>29 min. read</span>
    </p>

    <div class="hero-image-container">
        <picture><source srcset="https://web-assets.esetstatic.com/tn/-x266/wls/2023/2023-8/moustache-bouncer-1920x1080.jpeg" media="(max-width: 768px)" /><source srcset="https://web-assets.esetstatic.com/tn/-x425/wls/2023/2023-8/moustache-bouncer-1920x1080.jpeg" media="(max-width: 1120px)" /><img class="hero-image"  src="https://web-assets.esetstatic.com/tn/-x700/wls/2023/2023-8/moustache-bouncer-1920x1080.jpeg" alt="MoustachedBouncer: Espionage against foreign diplomats in Belarus"  /></picture>    </div>
</div>
                    <div class="article-body">
    <p>MoustachedBouncer is a cyberespionage group discovered by ESET Research and first publicly disclosed in this blogpost. The group has been active since at least 2014 and only targets foreign embassies in Belarus. Since 2020, MoustachedBouncer has most likely been able to perform <a href="https://attack.mitre.org/versions/v11/techniques/T1557/"><em>adversary-in-the-middle</em></a> (AitM) attacks at the ISP level, within Belarus, in order to compromise its targets. The group uses two separate toolsets that we have named NightClub and Disco.</p>
<blockquote>
<p><strong>Key points of this report:</strong></p>
<ul>
<li><em>MoustachedBouncer has been operating since at least 2014.</em></li>
<li><em>We assess with medium confidence that they are aligned with Belarus's interests.</em></li>
<li><em>MoustachedBouncer specializes in the espionage of foreign embassies in Belarus.</em></li>
<li><em>MoustachedBouncer has used the adversary-in-the-middle technique since 2020 to redirect captive portal checks to a C&amp;C server and deliver malware plugins via SMB shares.</em></li>
<li><em>We believe that MoustachedBouncer uses a lawful interception system (such as SORM) to conduct its AitM operations.</em></li>
<li><em>We assess with low confidence that MoustachedBouncer is closely cooperating with Winter Vivern, another group targeting European diplomats but using different TTPs.</em></li>
<li><em>Since 2014, the group has been operating a malware framework that we have named NightClub. It uses the SMTP and IMAP (email) protocols for C&amp;C communications.</em></li>
<li><em>Starting in 2020, the group has been using, in parallel, a second malware framework we have named Disco.</em></li>
<li><em>Both NightClub and Disco support additional spying plugins including a screenshotter, an audio recorder, and a file stealer.</em></li>
</ul>
</blockquote>
<blockquote>
<p>The group's intricate tactics, techniques and procedures were also discussed on the ESET Research Podcast. Just press play to learn more from ESET's Director of Threat Research Jean-Ian Boutin and ESET Distinguished Researcher Aryeh Goretsky.&nbsp;</p>
<p><iframe style="border: none; min-width: min(100%, 430px); height: 150px;" title="MoustachedBouncer" src="https://www.podbean.com/player-v2/?i=guxcg-14764d5-pb&amp;from=pb6admin&amp;share=1&amp;download=1&amp;rtl=0&amp;fonts=Arial&amp;skin=f6f6f6&amp;font-color=auto&amp;logo_link=episode_page&amp;btn-skin=0096a1" width="100%" height="150" scrolling="no" loading="lazy" data-name="pb-iframe-player"></iframe></p>
</blockquote>
<h2>Victimology</h2>
<p>According to ESET telemetry, the group targets foreign embassies in Belarus, and we have identified four different countries whose embassy staff have been targeted: two from Europe, one from South Asia, and one from Africa. The key dates are shown in Figure 1.</p>
<figure class="image"><img title="Figure 1. Timeline of MoustachedBouncer activities" src="https://web-assets.esetstatic.com/wls/2023/2023-8/moustachedbouncer-timeline-edited.png" alt="MoustachedBouncer_Timeline_edited" width="" height="" />
<figcaption>

<h2>Attribution</h2>
<p>While we track MoustachedBouncer as a separate group, we have found elements that make us assess with low confidence that they are closely collaborating with another group known as Winter Vivern. The latter was <a href="https://www.domaintools.com/resources/blog/winter-vivern-a-look-at-re-crafted-government-maldocs/"><em>discovered</em></a> in 2021 and is still active as of 2023. In March 2023, Winter Vivern used a known XSS vulnerability (<a href="https://nvd.nist.gov/vuln/detail/CVE-2022-27926"><em>CVE-2022-27926</em></a>) in the Zimbra mail portal in order to steal webmail credentials of diplomats of several European countries. This campaign was publicly disclosed by <a href="https://www.proofpoint.com/us/blog/threat-insight/exploitation-dish-best-served-cold-winter-vivern-uses-known-zimbra-vulnerability"><em>Proofpoint</em></a> researchers.</p>
<p>MoustachedBouncer&rsquo;s activity spans from 2014 to 2022 and the TTPs of the group have evolved over time. For example, we have first seen them use AitM attacks only in 2020. However, the targeted vertical has stayed the same.</p>
<p>Table 1 shows the characteristics of each campaign. Given these elements, we assess with high confidence that they are all linked to MoustachedBouncer.</p>
<p class="MsoCaption" style="break-after: avoid; text-align: center;"><em><a name="_Ref98857958"></a><span lang="EN-US">Table </span><!-- [if supportFields]><span style='mso-bookmark:
_Ref98857958'></span><span style='mso-element:field-begin'></span><span
style='mso-bookmark:_Ref98857958'><span lang=EN-US><span

<div align="center">&nbsp;</div>
<h2>Compromise vector: AitM</h2>
<p>In this section, we detail the initial access for Disco. We don&rsquo;t yet know the initial access method MoustachedBouncer uses to install NightClub.</p>
<h3>Fake Windows Update</h3>
<p>To compromise their targets, MoustachedBouncer operators tamper with their victims&rsquo; internet access, probably at the ISP level, to make Windows believe it&rsquo;s behind a captive portal. <a href="https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/troubleshoot/windows-client/networking/internet-explorer-edge-open-connect-corporate-public-network"><em>Windows 10 checks</em></a> whether it&rsquo;s able to access the internet with an HTTP request to <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">http://www.msftconnecttest.com/connecttest.txt</span>. In case the answer is not <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">Microsoft Connect Test</span>, a browser window is opened to <span style="font-family: courier new, courier, monospace;">http://www.msftconnecttest.com/redirect</span> . For IP ranges targeted by MoustachedBouncer, the network traffic is tampered at the ISP level, and the latter URL redirects to a seemingly legitimate, but fake, Windows Update URL, <span style="font-family: courier new, courier, monospace;">http://updates.microsoft[.]com/</span>. Hence, the fake Windows Update page will be displayed to a potential victim upon network connection. The fake update page is shown in Figure 2. The text we observed is in Russian, most likely because that is the main language used in Belarus, but it is possible that versions in other languages exist. The page indicates that there are critical system security updates that must be installed.</p>
<figure class="image"><img title="Figure 2. Fake Windows Update page" src="https://web-assets.esetstatic.com/wls/2023/2023-8/picture1.png" alt="Figure 2. Fake Windows Update page" width="" height="" />
<figcaption>
<p class="MsoCaption" style="margin: 6.0pt 0cm 6.0pt 0cm;"><em><a name="_Ref98854757"></a><span lang="EN-US">Figure </span><!-- [if supportFields]><span
style='mso-bookmark:_Ref98854757'></span><span style='mso-element:field-begin'></span><span
style='mso-bookmark:_Ref98854757'><span lang=EN-US><span
style='mso-spacerun:yes'> </span>SEQ Figure \* ARABIC <span style='mso-element:
field-separator'></span></span></span><![endif]--><span lang="EN-US">2</span><!-- [if supportFields]><span
style='mso-bookmark:_Ref98854757'></span><span style='mso-element:field-end'></span><![endif]--></em><span lang="EN-US"><em>. Fake Windows Update page</em></span></p>
</figcaption>
</figure>
<p>Note that it is using unencrypted HTTP and not HTTPS, and that the <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">updates.microsoft[.]com</span> subdomain does not exist on Microsoft&rsquo;s nameservers, so it does not resolve on the open internet. During the attack, this domain resolved to <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">5.45.121[.]106</span> on the target&rsquo;s machine. This IP address is used for parking domains and is unrelated to Microsoft. Although this is an internet-routable IP address, traffic to this IP never reaches the internet while the AitM attack is ongoing. Both the DNS resolutions and the HTTP replies were injected in transit, probably at the ISP level.</p>
<p>An important point is that the adversary-in-the-middle (AitM) technique only occurs against a few selected organizations (perhaps just embassies), not countrywide. It is not possible to reproduce the redirection by simply exiting from a random IP address in Belarus.</p>
<h3>Malware delivery</h3>
<p>The HTML page, shown in Figure 2, loads JavaScript code from <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">http://updates.microsoft[.]com/jdrop.js</span>. This script first calls <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">setTimeout</span> to execute the function <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">jdrop</span> one second after the page has loaded. That function (see Figure 3) displays a modal window with a button named <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">Получить обновления</span> (translation: Get updates).</p>
<figure class="image"><img title="Figure 3. jdrop function" src="https://web-assets.esetstatic.com/wls/2023/2023-8/jdrop.png" alt="Figure 3. jdrop function" width="" height="" />
<figcaption>
<p class="MsoCaption"><em><a name="_Ref100231434"></a><span lang="EN-US">Figure </span><!-- [if supportFields]><span
style='mso-bookmark:_Ref100231434'></span><span style='mso-element:field-begin'></span><span
style='mso-bookmark:_Ref100231434'><span lang=EN-US><span
style='mso-spacerun:yes'> </span>SEQ Figure \* ARABIC <span style='mso-element:
field-separator'></span></span></span><![endif]--><span lang="EN-US">3</span><!-- [if supportFields]><span
style='mso-bookmark:_Ref100231434'></span><span style='mso-element:field-end'></span><![endif]--><span lang="EN-US">. </span><span class="Code"><span lang="EN-US">jdrop</span></span></em><span lang="EN-US"><em> function</em></span></p>
</figcaption>
</figure>
<p>A click on the button executes the <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">update</span> function, shown in Figure 4.</p>
<figure class="image"><img title="Figure 4. update function" src="https://web-assets.esetstatic.com/wls/2023/2023-8/update-js.png" alt="Figure 4. update function" width="" height="" />
<figcaption>
<p class="MsoCaption"><em><a name="_Ref100231772"></a><span lang="EN-US">Figure </span><!-- [if supportFields]><span
style='mso-bookmark:_Ref100231772'></span><span style='mso-element:field-begin'></span><span
style='mso-bookmark:_Ref100231772'><span lang=EN-US><span
style='mso-spacerun:yes'> </span>SEQ Figure \* ARABIC <span style='mso-element:
field-separator'></span></span></span><![endif]--><span lang="EN-US">4</span><!-- [if supportFields]><span
style='mso-bookmark:_Ref100231772'></span><span style='mso-element:field-end'></span><![endif]--><span lang="EN-US">. </span><span class="Code"><span lang="EN-US">update</span></span></em><span lang="EN-US"><em> function</em></span></p>
</figcaption>
</figure>
<p>This function triggers the download of a fake Windows Update installer from the legitimate-seeming URL <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">http://updates.microsoft[.]com/MicrosoftUpdate845255.zip</span>. It also displays some instructions to install the update: <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">Для установки обновлений, скачайте и запустите "MicrosoftUpdate845255.msi"</span>. (translation: To install updates, download and run "MicrosoftUpdate845255.msi").</p>
<p>We were unable to retrieve the downloaded <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">MicrosoftUpdate845255.zip</span> file but our telemetry shows it contains a malicious executable named <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">MicrosoftUpdate845255.exe</span>.</p>
<p>Written in Go, it creates a scheduled task that executes <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">\\35.214.56[.]2\OfficeBroker\OfficeBroker.exe</span> every minute. Like the path suggests, it fetches the executable via SMB from <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">35.214.56[.]2</span>. This IP address belongs to a Google Cloud customer, but just like the HTTP server, we believe that SMB replies are injected on the fly via AitM and that the attackers don&rsquo;t control the actual internet-routable IP address.</p>
<p>We have also observed the following SMB servers, intercepted via AitM:</p>
<ul>
<li><span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">\\209.19.37[.]184</span></li>
<li><span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">\\38.9.8[.]78</span></li>
<li><span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">\\59.6.8[.]25</span></li>
</ul>
<p>We have observed this behavior in two separate ISP networks: Unitary Enterprise A1 and Beltelecom. This suggests that those ISPs may not provide full data confidentiality and integrity. We strongly recommend that foreign organizations in Belarus use an end-to-end encrypted VPN tunnel, ideally out-of-band (i.e., not from the endpoint), providing internet connectivity from a trusted network.</p>
<p>Figure 5 depicts our hypothesis about the compromise vector and the traffic interception.</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<figure class="image"><img title="Figure 5. Compromise via AitM scenario" src="https://web-assets.esetstatic.com/wls/2023/2023-8/slide1.jpeg" alt="Figure 5. Compromise via AitM scenario" width="" height="" />
<figcaption>
<p class="MsoCaption"><em><a name="_Ref100237603"></a><span lang="EN-US">Figure </span><!-- [if supportFields]><span
style='mso-bookmark:_Ref100237603'></span><span style='mso-element:field-begin'></span><span
style='mso-bookmark:_Ref100237603'><span lang=EN-US><span
style='mso-spacerun:yes'> </span>SEQ Figure \* ARABIC <span style='mso-element:
field-separator'></span></span></span><![endif]--><span lang="EN-US">5</span><!-- [if supportFields]><span
style='mso-bookmark:_Ref100237603'></span><span style='mso-element:field-end'></span><![endif]--></em><span lang="EN-US"><em>. Compromise via AitM scenario</em></span></p>
</figcaption>
</figure>
<h3>AitM &ndash; General thoughts</h3>
<p>The AitM scenario reminds us of the Turla and StrongPity threat actors who have trojanized software installers on the fly at the ISP level.&nbsp;</p>
<p>Usually, this initial access method is used by threat actors operating in their own country because it requires significant access inside the internet service providers, or their upstream providers. In many countries, security services are allowed to perform so-called &ldquo;lawful interception&rdquo; using special devices installed on the ISPs&rsquo; premises.</p>
<p>In Russia, a law from 2014 requires ISPs to install devices called <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/SORM"><em>SORM-3</em></a> that enable the Federal Security Service (FSB) to <a href="https://www.csis.org/analysis/reference-note-russian-communications-surveillance"><em>conduct targeted surveillance</em></a>. The devices have deep packet inspection (DPI) capabilities and were likely used by Turla in its <a href="https://www.welivesecurity.com/wp-content/uploads/2018/01/ESET_Turla_Mosquito.pdf"><em>Mosquito campaign</em></a>.</p>
<p>In 2018, the Citizen Lab revealed that DPI devices developed by the Canadian company Sandvine were used to modify HTTP traffic in Turkey and Egypt. In Turkey, the devices were allegedly used to redirect internet users to a malicious server when they tried to download certain Windows applications, which is in line with StrongPity activities. In Egypt, those devices were allegedly used to inject ads and cryptocurrency mining scripts in order to generate money.</p>
<p>In 2020, a <a href="https://www.bloomberg.com/news/articles/2020-08-28/belarusian-officials-shut-down-internet-with-technology-made-by-u-s-firm" target="_blank" rel="noopener"><em>Bloomberg article</em></a> revealed that Belarus&rsquo;s National Traffic Exchange Center bought the same Sandvine DPI equipment, but according to a <a href="https://www.cyberscoop.com/sandvine-belarus-contract-censorship-human-rights/"><em>Cyberscoop article</em></a> the contract was cancelled in September 2020.</p>
<p>According to a <a href="https://www.amnesty.org/en/wp-content/uploads/2021/05/EUR4990452018ENGLISH.pdf" target="_blank" rel="noopener"><em>report by Amnesty International</em></a> published in 2021, &ldquo;Under Belarusian law, all telecommunications providers in the country must make their hardware compatible with the SORM system&rdquo;. They also state that &ldquo;The SORM system allows the authorities direct, remote-control access to all user communications and associated data without notifying the provider&rdquo;. We assess with low confidence that MoustachedBouncer uses this SORM system to conduct its operations.</p>
<p>While the compromise of routers in order to conduct AitM on embassy networks cannot be fully discarded, the presence of lawful interception capabilities in Belarus suggests the traffic mangling is happening at the ISP level rather than on the targets&rsquo; routers.</p>
<h2>Implants: NightClub and Disco</h2>
<p>Since 2014, the malware families used by MoustachedBouncer have evolved, and a big change happened in 2020 when the group started to use AitM attacks. At the same time, it started to use much simpler tools developed in .NET and Go. In reference to NightClub, we named this new toolset Disco.</p>
<p>MoustachedBouncer operates the two implant families in parallel, but on a given machine, only one is deployed at a time. We believe that Disco is used in conjunction with AitM attacks while NightClub is used for victims where traffic interception at the ISP level isn&rsquo;t possible because of a mitigation such as the use of an end-to-end encrypted VPN where internet traffic is routed outside of Belarus.</p>
<h3>Disco</h3>
<p>As mentioned in the previous section, a fake Windows Update page delivers the first stage (SHA-1: <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">E65EB4467DDB1C99B09AE87BA0A964C36BAB4C30</span>). This is a simple dropper written in Go that creates a scheduled task to execute <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">\\35.214.56[.]2\OfficeBroker\OfficeBroker.exe</span> every minute. <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">OfficeBroker.exe</span> is downloaded over the SMB protocol via AitM attack. The dropper&rsquo;s main function is shown in Figure 6.</p>
<figure class="image"><img title="Figure 6. Main function of the Go dropper" src="https://web-assets.esetstatic.com/wls/2023/2023-8/figure-6.png" alt="Figure 6. Main function of the Go dropper" width="" height="" />
<figcaption>
<p class="MsoCaption"><em><a name="_Ref99634114"></a><span lang="EN-US">Figure </span><!-- [if supportFields]><span
style='mso-bookmark:_Ref99634114'></span><span style='mso-element:field-begin'></span><span
style='mso-bookmark:_Ref99634114'><span lang=EN-US><span
style='mso-spacerun:yes'> </span>SEQ Figure \* ARABIC <span style='mso-element:
field-separator'></span></span></span><![endif]--><span lang="EN-US">6</span><!-- [if supportFields]><span
style='mso-bookmark:_Ref99634114'></span><span style='mso-element:field-end'></span><![endif]--></em><span lang="EN-US"><em>. Main function of the Go dropper</em></span></p>
</figcaption>
</figure>
<p>Finally, the dropper does a DNS query for <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">windows.system.update[.]com</span>. This domain does not exist but the DNS request is probably intercepted via AitM, and is likely a beacon to notify the operators that the machine has been successfully compromised.</p>
<p>We were unable to retrieve the <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">OfficeBroker.exe</span> file, but it is very likely that it acts as a downloader, since we have observed further plugins being executed from SMB shares. The plugins are developed in Go and are rather simple because they mostly rely on external Go libraries. Table 2 summarizes the different plugins.</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>

<p>Interestingly, the plugins also use SMB shares for data exfiltration. There is no C&amp;C server outside the attackers&rsquo; premises to look at or to take down. There also seems to be no way to reach that C&amp;C server from the internet. This gives high resiliency to the attackers&rsquo; network infrastructure.</p>
<h3>SharpDisco and NightClub plugins</h3>
<p>In January 2020 we observed a MoustachedBouncer dropper, which we named SharpDisco, being downloaded from <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">https://mail.mfa.gov.&lt;redacted&gt;/EdgeUpdate.exe</span> by a Microsoft Edge process. It is not clear how attackers were able to tamper with HTTPS traffic, but it is possible an invalid TLS certificate warning was shown to the victim. Another possibility is that MoustachedBouncer compromised this governmental website.</p>
<h4>SharpDisco (SHA-1: A3AE82B19FEE2756D6354E85A094F1A4598314AB)</h4>
<p>SharpDisco is a dropper developed in C#. It displays a fake update window, shown in Figure 7, while creating two scheduled tasks in the background.</p>
<figure class="image"><img title="Figure 7. Fake Microsoft Edge update window" src="https://web-assets.esetstatic.com/wls/2023/2023-8/edge-update.png" alt="Figure 7. Fake Microsoft Edge update window" width="" height="" />
<figcaption>
<p class="MsoCaption"><em><a name="_Ref99640194"></a><span lang="EN-US">Figure </span><!-- [if supportFields]><span
style='mso-bookmark:_Ref99640194'></span><span style='mso-element:field-begin'></span><span
style='mso-bookmark:_Ref99640194'><span lang=EN-US><span
style='mso-spacerun:yes'> </span>SEQ Figure \* ARABIC <span style='mso-element:
field-separator'></span></span></span><![endif]--><span lang="EN-US">7</span><!-- [if supportFields]><span
style='mso-bookmark:_Ref99640194'></span><span style='mso-element:field-end'></span><![endif]--></em><span lang="EN-US"><em>. Fake Microsoft Edge update window</em></span></p>
</figcaption>
</figure>
<p>These scheduled tasks are:</p>
<p><img style="display: block; margin-left: auto; margin-right: auto;" title="" src="https://web-assets.esetstatic.com/wls/2023/2023-8/scheduled-tasks.png" alt="scheduled tasks" width="" height="" /></p>
<p><span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">WINCMDA.EXE</span> and <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">WINCMDB.EXE</span> are probably just <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">cmd.exe</span> renamed. Every minute, the task reads what is in <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">\\24.9.51[.]94\EDGEUPDATE\EDGEAIN</span> (on the SMB share), pipes it to <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">cmd.exe</span>, and writes the output to <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">\\24.9.51[.]94\EDGEUPDATE\EDGEAOUT</span>. It is the same for the second task, but with the <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">EDGEBIN</span> and <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">EDGEBOUT</span> files. From a higher viewpoint, those tasks are reverse shells with a one-second latency.</p>
<p>Then, as shown in Figure 8, the dropper sends a DNS request for an unregistered domain, <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">edgeupdate-security-windows[.]com</span>. This is similar to what the 2022 Disco dropper does.</p>
<figure class="image"><img title="Figure 8. Dropper used in 2020" src="https://web-assets.esetstatic.com/wls/2023/2023-8/dropper-2020.png" alt="Figure 8. Dropper used in 2020" width="" height="" />
<figcaption>
<p class="MsoCaption"><em><a name="_Ref99640740"></a><span lang="EN-US">Figure </span><!-- [if supportFields]><span
style='mso-bookmark:_Ref99640740'></span><span style='mso-element:field-begin'></span><span
style='mso-bookmark:_Ref99640740'><span lang=EN-US><span
style='mso-spacerun:yes'> </span>SEQ Figure \* ARABIC <span style='mso-element:
field-separator'></span></span></span><![endif]--><span lang="EN-US">8</span><!-- [if supportFields]><span
style='mso-bookmark:_Ref99640740'></span><span style='mso-element:field-end'></span><![endif]--></em><span lang="EN-US"><em>. Dropper used in 2020</em></span></p>
</figcaption>
</figure>
<p>ESET telemetry shows that the reverse shell was used to drop a genuine Python interpreter in <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">C:\Users\Public\WinTN\WinTN.exe</span>. We then observed two plugins being dropped on disk by cmd.exe, which means they were likely dropped by the reverse shell as well. The two plugins are:</p>
<ul>
<li>A recent-files stealer in <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">C:\Users\Public\WinSrcNT\It11.exe</span></li>
<li>An external drive monitor in <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">C:\Users\Public\It3.exe</span></li>
</ul>
<p>It is interesting to note that those plugins share code with NightClub (described in the section NightClub &ndash; 2017 (SHA-1: <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">F92FE4DD679903F75ADE64DC8A20D46DFBD3B277</span>) below). This allowed us to link the Disco and NightClub toolsets.</p>
<h4>Recent-files stealer (SHA-1: 0DAEA89F91A55F46D33C294CFE84EF06CE22E393)</h4>
<p>This plugin is a Windows executable named <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">It11.exe</span>. We believe it was executed via the reverse shell mentioned above. There is no persistence mechanism implemented in the plugin.</p>
<p>It gets the files recently opened on the machine by reading the content of the folder <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">%USERPROFILE%\Recent</span> (on Windows XP) or of <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">%APPDATA%\Microsoft\Windows\Recent</span> (in newer Windows versions). Those folders contain LNK files, each pointing to a recently opened file.</p>
<p>The plugin embeds its own LNK format parser in order to extract the path to the original file.</p>
<p>We were unable to make this plugin work, but static analysis shows that the files are exfiltrated to the SMB share <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">\\24.9.51[.]94\EDGEUPDATE\update\</span>. The plugin maintains a list of already exfiltrated files, and their CRC-32 checksum, in <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">%TEMP%\index.dat</span>. This likely avoids retransmitting the same file more than once.</p>
<h4>External drive monitor (SHA-1: 11CF38D971534D9B619581CEDC19319962F3B996)</h4>
<p>This plugin is a Windows executable named <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">It3.exe</span>. As with the recent-files stealer, it doesn&rsquo;t implement any persistence mechanism.</p>
<p>The plugin calls <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">GetLogicalDrives</span> in a loop to get a list of all connected drives, including removable ones such as USB keys. Then, it does a raw copy of the NTFS volume of each removable drive and writes it in the current working directory, <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">C:\Users\Public\</span> in our example. The filename is a randomly generated string of six to eight alphanumeric characters, for example <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">heNNYwmY</span>.</p>
<p>It maintains a log file in <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">&lt;working directory&gt;\index.dat</span> with the CRC-32 checksums of the copied disks.</p>
<p>The plugin doesn&rsquo;t appear to have any exfiltration capabilities. It is likely that the staged drive dumps are later retrieved using the reverse shell.</p>
<h3>NightClub</h3>
<p>Since 2014, MoustachedBouncer has been using a malware framework we named NightClub because it contains a C++ class named <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">nightclub</span>. We found samples from 2014, 2017, 2020, and 2022. This section describes the evolution of NightClub from a simple backdoor to a fully modular C++ implant.</p>
<p>In summary, NightClub is an implant family using emails for its C&amp;C communications. Since 2016, additional modules could be delivered by email to extend its spying capabilities.</p>
<h4>NightClub &ndash; 2014</h4>
<p>This is the oldest known version of NightClub. We found a dropper and an orchestrator.</p>
<p>The dropper (SHA-1: <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">0401EE7F3BC384734BF7E352C4C4BC372840C30D</span>) is an executable named <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">EsetUpdate-0117583943.exe</span>, and it was uploaded to VirusTotal from Ukraine on 2014-11-19. We don&rsquo;t know how it was distributed at that time.</p>
<p>The main function, illustrated in Figure 9, loads the resource <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">MEMORY</span> and writes its content in <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">%SystemRoot%\System32\creh.dll</span>. It is stored in cleartext in the PE resource.</p>
<figure class="image"><img title="Figure 9. Main function of the dropper" src="https://web-assets.esetstatic.com/wls/2023/2023-8/2014-main-dropper.png" alt="Figure 9. Main function of the dropper" width="" height="" />
<figcaption>
<p class="MsoCaption"><em><a name="_Ref100137570"></a><span lang="EN-US">Figure </span><!-- [if supportFields]><span
style='mso-bookmark:_Ref100137570'></span><span style='mso-element:field-begin'></span><span
style='mso-bookmark:_Ref100137570'><span lang=EN-US><span
style='mso-spacerun:yes'> </span>SEQ Figure \* ARABIC <span style='mso-element:
field-separator'></span></span></span><![endif]--><span lang="EN-US">9</span><!-- [if supportFields]><span
style='mso-bookmark:_Ref100137570'></span><span style='mso-element:field-end'></span><![endif]--></em><span lang="EN-US"><em>. Main function of the dropper</em></span></p>
</figcaption>
</figure>
<p>Then, the dropper modifies the Creation, Access, and Write timestamps of <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">creh.dll</span> to those of the genuine Windows DLL <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">user32.dll.</span></p>
<p>Finally, it creates a Windows service named <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">WmdmPmSp</span> and sets, in the registry, its <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">ServiceDll</span> to <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">%SystemRoot%\System32\creh.dll &ndash;</span> see Figure 10.</p>
<figure class="image"><img title="Figure 10. Modification of the value ServiceDll" src="https://web-assets.esetstatic.com/wls/2023/2023-8/2014-servicedll.png" alt="Figure 10. Modification of the value ServiceDll" width="" height="" />
<figcaption>
<p class="MsoCaption"><em><a name="_Ref100138685"></a><span lang="EN-US">Figure </span><!-- [if supportFields]><span

style='mso-bookmark:_Ref100138685'><span lang=EN-US><span

</figcaption>
</figure>
<p>The previously dropped DLL, <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">creh.dll</span> (SHA-1: <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">5B55250CC0DA407201B5F042322CFDBF56041632</span>) is the NightClub orchestrator. It has a single export named <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">ServiceMain</span> and its PDB path is <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">D:\Programming\Projects\Work\SwampThing\Release\Win32\WorkingDll.pdb.</span></p>
<p>It is written in C++ and the names of some methods and classes are present in the RTTI data &ndash; see Figure 11.</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<figure class="image"><img title="Figure 11. Method and class names from the RTTI data" src="https://web-assets.esetstatic.com/wls/2023/2023-8/figure-11.png" alt="Figure 11. Method and class names from the RTTI data" width="" height="" />
<figcaption>
<p class="MsoCaption"><em><a name="_Ref102485664"></a><span lang="EN-US">Figure </span><!-- [if supportFields]><span
style='mso-bookmark:_Ref102485664'></span><span style='mso-element:field-begin'></span><span

style='mso-spacerun:yes'> </span>SEQ Figure \* ARABIC <span style='mso-element:

style='mso-bookmark:_Ref102485664'></span><span style='mso-element:field-end'></span><![endif]--></em><span lang="EN-US"><em>. Method and class names from the RTTI data</em></span></p>
</figcaption>
</figure>
<p>Some of the strings are encrypted using the following linear congruential generator (LCG): <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">staten+1 = (690069 &times; staten + 1) mod 232</span>. For each encrypted string, a seed (state0) between 0 and 255 is provided. To decrypt a string, the <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">staten</span> is subtracted from each encrypted <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">byten</span>. An example of an encrypted string structure is shown in Figure 12.</p>
<figure class="image"><img title="Figure 12. Encrypted string format" src="https://web-assets.esetstatic.com/wls/2023/2023-8/2014-encrypted-string.png" alt="Figure 12. Encrypted string format" width="" height="" />
<figcaption>
<p class="MsoCaption"><em><a name="_Ref100139630"></a><span lang="EN-US">Figure </span><!-- [if supportFields]><span
style='mso-bookmark:_Ref100139630'></span><span style='mso-element:field-begin'></span><span
style='mso-bookmark:_Ref100139630'><span lang=EN-US><span
style='mso-spacerun:yes'> </span>SEQ Figure \* ARABIC <span style='mso-element:
field-separator'></span></span></span><![endif]--><span lang="EN-US">12</span><!-- [if supportFields]><span
style='mso-bookmark:_Ref100139630'></span><span style='mso-element:field-end'></span><![endif]--></em><span lang="EN-US"><em>. Encrypted string format</em></span></p>
</figcaption>
</figure>
<p>A non-encrypted log file is present in <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">C:\Windows\System32\servdll.log</span>. It contains very basic information about the initialization of the orchestrator &ndash; see Figure 13.</p>
<figure class="image"><img title="Figure 13. Log file" src="https://web-assets.esetstatic.com/wls/2023/2023-8/2014-log-file.png" alt="Figure 13. Log file" width="" height="" />
<figcaption>
<p class="MsoCaption"><a name="_Ref100148448"></a><em><span lang="EN-US">Figure </span><!-- [if supportFields]><span

</figcaption>
</figure>
<p>NightClub has two main capabilities:</p>
<p>&bull;<span style="white-space: pre;"> </span>Monitoring files</p>
<p>&bull;<span style="white-space: pre;"> </span>Exfiltrating data via SMTP (email)</p>
<h5>File monitor</h5>
<p>Functionality implemented here is very close to that of the recent file monitor plugin seen in 2020 and described above. It also browses the directories <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">%USERPROFILE%\Recent</span> on Windows XP, and in newer Windows versions <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">%APPDATA%\Microsoft\Windows\Recent</span>, and implements the same LNK parser &ndash; see Figure 14 and Figure 15.</p>
<figure class="image"><img title="Figure 14. LNK parser (2014 sample &ndash; 5B55250CC0DA407201B5F042322CFDBF56041632)" src="https://web-assets.esetstatic.com/wls/2023/2023-8/2014-lnk-parser.png" alt="Figure 14. LNK parser (2014 sample &ndash; 5B55250CC0DA407201B5F042322CFDBF56041632)" width="" height="" />
<figcaption>
<p class="MsoCaption"><em><a name="_Ref102485727"></a><span lang="EN-US">Figure </span><!-- [if supportFields]><span

style='mso-bookmark:_Ref102485727'><span lang=EN-US><span
style='mso-spacerun:yes'> </span>SEQ Figure \* ARABIC <span style='mso-element:

style='mso-bookmark:_Ref102485727'></span><span style='mso-element:field-end'></span><![endif]--><span lang="EN-US">. LNK parser (2014 sample &ndash; </span><span class="Code"><span lang="EN-US">5B55250CC0DA407201B5F042322CFDBF56041632</span></span></em><span lang="EN-US"><em>)</em></span></p>
</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="image"><img title="Figure 15. LNK parser (2020 sample &ndash; 0DAEA89F91A55F46D33C294CFE84EF06CE22E393)" src="https://web-assets.esetstatic.com/wls/2023/2023-8/2020-lnk-parser-1.png" alt="Figure 15. </figcaption>
</figure>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<p>The files retrieved from the LNK files are copied to <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">%TEMP%\&lt;original filename&gt;.bin</span>. Note that unlike the 2020 variant, only files with extensions <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">.doc</span>, <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">.docx</span>, <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">.xls</span>, <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">.xslx</span>, or <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">.pdf</span> are copied.</p>
<p>It also monitors removable drives in a loop, in order to steal files from them.</p>
<h5>SMTP C&amp;C communications</h5>
<p>NightClub uses the SMTP protocol to exfiltrate data. Even if C&amp;C communication by email is not unique to MoustachedBouncer and is also used by other adversaries such as Turla (see <a href="https://web-assets.esetstatic.com/wls/2019/05/ESET-LightNeuron.pdf">LightNeuron</a> and the <a href="https://web-assets.esetstatic.com/wls/2018/08/Eset-Turla-Outlook-Backdoor.pdf">Outlook</a> backdoor), it is quite rare. The code is based on the CSmtp project available on <a href="https://github.com/korisk/csmtp/blob/master/CSmtp.cpp">GitHub</a>. The email accounts&rsquo; information is hardcoded, encrypted with the LCG algorithm. In the sample we analyzed, the mail configuration is:</p>
<p>&bull;<span style="white-space: pre;"> </span><strong>SMTP server</strong>: <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">smtp.seznam.cz</span></p>
<p>&bull;<span style="white-space: pre;"> </span><strong>Sender address</strong>: <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">glen.morriss75@seznam[.]cz</span></p>
<p>&bull;<span style="white-space: pre;"> </span><strong>Sender password</strong>: &lt;redacted&gt;</p>
<p>&bull;<span style="white-space: pre;"> </span><strong>Recipient address</strong>: <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">SunyaF@seznam[.]cz</span></p>
<p>seznam.cz is a Czech web portal offering a free webmail service. We believe the attackers created their own email accounts, instead of compromising legitimate ones.</p>
<p>NightClub exfiltrates the files previously copied to <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">%TEMP%</span> by the file monitor functionality (<span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">FileMonitor</span> in Figure 11). They&rsquo;re encoded in base64 and added as an attachment. The attachment name is the original filename with the .bin extension.</p>
<p>Figure 16 shows the exfiltration of a file via SMTP. NightClub authenticates using the credentials for the&nbsp; <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">glen.morriss75@seznam[.]cz</span> account and sends an email to <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">SunyaF@seznam[.]cz</span> with the stolen file attached.</p>
<figure class="image"><img title="Figure 16. TCP stream of the SMTP communication from our test machine" src="https://web-assets.esetstatic.com/wls/2023/2023-8/figure16.png" alt="Figure 16. TCP stream of the SMTP communication from our test machine" width="" height="" />
<figcaption>
<p class="MsoCaption"><em><a name="_Ref100153104"></a><span lang="EN-US">Figure </span><!-- [if supportFields]><span
style='mso-bookmark:_Ref100153104'></span><span style='mso-element:field-begin'></span><span
style='mso-bookmark:_Ref100153104'><span lang=EN-US><span
style='mso-spacerun:yes'> </span>SEQ Figure \* ARABIC <span style='mso-element:
field-separator'></span></span></span><![endif]--><span lang="EN-US">16</span><!-- [if supportFields]><span
style='mso-bookmark:_Ref100153104'></span><span style='mso-element:field-end'></span><![endif]--></em><span lang="EN-US"><em>. TCP stream of the SMTP communication from our test machine</em></span></p>
</figcaption>
</figure>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<p>Note that some headers that might look suspicious at first sight are the defaults from the CSmtp project, so they are probably not distinctive. These include:</p>
<p>&bull;<span style="white-space: pre;"> </span><span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">X-Mailer: The Bat! (v3.02) Professional</span></p>
<p>&bull;<span style="white-space: pre;"> </span><span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">Content-Type: multipart/mixed; boundary="__MESSAGE__ID__54yg6f6h6y456345"</span></p>
<p>The Bat! is an email client widely used in Eastern Europe. As such, the <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">X-Mailer</span> header likely blends in with email traffic in Belarus.</p>
<h4>NightClub &ndash; 2017 (SHA-1: F92FE4DD679903F75ADE64DC8A20D46DFBD3B277)</h4>
<p>In 2017, we found a more recent version of NightClub, which was compiled on 2017-06-05. On the victim&rsquo;s machine, it was located at <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">C:\Windows\System32\metamn.dll</span>. Its filename in the DLL export directory is <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">DownloaderService.dll</span>, and it has a single export named <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">ServiceMain</span>. It contains the PDB path <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">D:\AbcdMainProject\Rootsrc\Projects\MainS\Ink\Release\x64\EtfFavoriteFinder.pdb.&nbsp;</span></p>
<p>To persist, it creates a Windows service named <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">WmdmPmSp</span>, as in previous versions. Unfortunately, we have not been able to recover the dropper.</p>
<p>This NightClub version also includes a few C++ class and method names, including <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">nightclub</span>, in the RTTI data &ndash; see Figure 17.</p>
<figure class="image"><img title="Figure 17. Method and class names from the RTTI data of the 2017 NightClub version" src="https://web-assets.esetstatic.com/wls/2023/2023-8/figure-17.png" alt="Figure 17. Method and class names from the RTTI data of the 2017 NightClub version" width="" height="" />
<figcaption>
<p class="MsoCaption"><em><a name="_Ref102559180"></a><span lang="EN-US">Figure </span><!-- [if supportFields]><span
style='mso-bookmark:_Ref102559180'></span><span style='mso-element:field-begin'></span><span
style='mso-bookmark:_Ref102559180'><span lang=EN-US><span
style='mso-spacerun:yes'> </span>SEQ Figure \* ARABIC <span style='mso-element:
field-separator'></span></span></span><![endif]--><span lang="EN-US">17</span><!-- [if supportFields]><span
style='mso-bookmark:_Ref102559180'></span><span style='mso-element:field-end'></span><![endif]--></em><span lang="EN-US"><em>. Method and class names from the RTTI data of the 2017 NightClub version</em></span></p>
</figcaption>
</figure>
<p>As in previous versions, C&amp;C communications use the SMTP protocol, via the CSmtp library, with hardcoded credentials. In the sample we analyzed, the mail configuration is:</p>
<p>&bull;<span style="white-space: pre;"> </span><strong>SMTP server</strong>: <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">smtp.mail.ru</span></p>
<p>&bull;<span style="white-space: pre;"> </span><strong>Sender address</strong>: <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">fhtgbbwi@mail[.]ru</span></p>
<p>&bull;<span style="white-space: pre;"> </span><strong>Sender password</strong>: [redacted]</p>
<p>&bull;<span style="white-space: pre;"> </span><strong>Recipient address</strong>: <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">nvjfnvjfnjf@mail[.]ru</span></p>
<p>The main difference is that they switched the free email provider from Seznam.cz to Mail.ru.</p>
<p>This NightClub version uses external plugins stored in the folder <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">%APPDATA%\NvmFilter\</span>. They are DLLs named <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">&lt;random&gt;.cr </span>(e.g., <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">et2z7q0FREZ.cr</span>) with a single export named <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">Starts</span>. We have identified two plugins: a keylogger and a file monitor.</p>
<h5>Keylogger (SHA-1: 6999730D0715606D14ACD19329AF0685B8AD0299)</h5>
<p>This plugin was stored in <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">%APPDATA%\NvmFilter\et2z7q0FREZ.cr</span> and is a DLL with one export, <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">Starts</span>. It contains the PDB path <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">D:\Programming\Projects\Autogen\Kh\AutogenAlg\Release\x64\SearchIdxDll.pdb</span> and was developed in C++. RTTI data shows a few class names &ndash; see Figure 18.</p>
<figure class="image"><img title="Figure 18. Method and class names from the RTTI data of the NightClub keylogger plugin" src="https://web-assets.esetstatic.com/wls/2023/2023-8/figure-18.png" alt="Figure 18. Method and class names from the RTTI data of the NightClub keylogger plugin" width="" height="" />
<figcaption>
<p class="MsoCaption"><em><a name="_Ref102559251"></a><span lang="EN-US">Figure </span><!-- [if supportFields]><span
style='mso-bookmark:_Ref102559251'></span><span style='mso-element:field-begin'></span><span
style='mso-bookmark:_Ref102559251'><span lang=EN-US><span
style='mso-spacerun:yes'> </span>SEQ Figure \* ARABIC <span style='mso-element:
field-separator'></span></span></span><![endif]--><span lang="EN-US">18</span><!-- [if supportFields]><span
style='mso-bookmark:_Ref102559251'></span><span style='mso-element:field-end'></span><![endif]--></em><span lang="EN-US"><em>. Method and class names from the RTTI data of the NightClub keylogger plugin</em></span></p>
</figcaption>
</figure>
<p>The keylogger implementation is rather traditional, using the Windows <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">GetKeyState</span> API function &ndash; see Figure 19.</p>
<figure class="image"><img title="Figure 19. NightClub keylogger" src="https://web-assets.esetstatic.com/wls/2023/2023-8/keylogger.png" alt="Figure 19. NightClub keylogger" width="" height="" />
<figcaption>
<p class="MsoCaption"><em><a name="_Ref100156272"></a><span lang="EN-US">Figure </span><!-- [if supportFields]><span
style='mso-bookmark:_Ref100156272'></span><span style='mso-element:field-begin'></span><span
style='mso-bookmark:_Ref100156272'><span lang=EN-US><span
style='mso-spacerun:yes'> </span>SEQ Figure \* ARABIC <span style='mso-element:
field-separator'></span></span></span><![endif]--><span lang="EN-US">19</span><!-- [if supportFields]><span
style='mso-bookmark:_Ref100156272'></span><span style='mso-element:field-end'></span><![endif]--></em><span lang="EN-US"><em>. NightClub keylogger</em></span></p>
</figcaption>
</figure>
<p>The keylogger maintains a cleartext log file in <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">%TEMP%\uirtl.tmp</span>. It contains the date, the title of the application, and the logged keystrokes for this specific application. An example, which we generated, is provided in Figure 20.</p>
<figure class="image"><img title="Figure 20. Example of the output of the keylogger (generated by us)" src="https://web-assets.esetstatic.com/wls/2023/2023-8/figure-20.png" alt="Figure 20. Example of the output of the keylogger (generated by us)" width="" height="" />
<figcaption>
<p class="MsoCaption"><em><a name="_Ref102486430"></a><span lang="EN-US">Figure </span><!-- [if supportFields]><span
style='mso-bookmark:_Ref102486430'></span><span style='mso-element:field-begin'></span><span
style='mso-bookmark:_Ref102486430'><span lang=EN-US><span
style='mso-spacerun:yes'> </span>SEQ Figure \* ARABIC <span style='mso-element:
field-separator'></span></span></span><![endif]--><span lang="EN-US">20</span><!-- [if supportFields]><span
style='mso-bookmark:_Ref102486430'></span><span style='mso-element:field-end'></span><![endif]--></em><span lang="EN-US"><em>. Example of the output of the keylogger (generated by us)</em></span></p>
</figcaption>
</figure>
<h5>File monitor (SHA-1: 6E729E84C7672F048ED8AE847F20A0219E917FA)</h5>
<p>This plugin was stored in <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">%APPDATA%\NvmFilter\sTUlsWa1.cr</span> and is a DLL with a single export named Starts. Its PDB path, <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">D:\Programming\Projects\Autogen\Kh\AutogenAlg\Release\x64\FileMonitoringModule.pdb</span>, has not been stripped, and it reuses code from the 2014 and 2020 file monitors, described above. It monitors drives and recent files, and copies files for exfiltration to <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">%TEMP%\AcmSym\rm</span>. Its log file is stored in <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">%TEMP%\indexwti.sxd.</span></p>
<h4>NightClub &ndash; 2020&ndash;2022</h4>
<p>In 2020-11, we observed a new version of NightClub deployed in Belarus, on the computers of the diplomatic staff of a European country. In 2022-07, MoustachedBouncer again compromised some of the same computers. The 2020 and 2022 versions of NightClub are almost identical, and the compromise vector remains unknown.</p>
<p>Its architecture is slightly different from the previous versions, as the orchestrator also implements networking functions. The second component, which its developers call the module agent, is only responsible for loading the plugins. All samples were found in the folder <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">%APPDATA%\microsoft\def\</span> and are written in C++ with statically linked libraries such as CSmtp or cpprestsdk. As a result, the executables are quite large &ndash; around 5MB.</p>
<h5>Orchestrator</h5>
<p>On the victims&rsquo; machines, both orchestrator variants (SHA-1: <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">92115E21E565440B1A26ECC20D2552A214155669</span> and <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">D14D9118335C9BF6633CB2A41023486DACBEB052</span>) were named <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">svhvost.exe</span>. We believe MoustachedBouncer tried to masquerade as the name of the legitimate executable <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">svchost.exe</span>. For persistence, it creates a service named <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">vAwast</span>.</p>
<p>Contrary to previous versions, to encrypt the strings they simply add <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">0x01</span> to each byte. For example, the string <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">cmd.exe</span> would be encrypted as <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">dne/fyf</span>. Another difference is that the configuration is stored in an external file, rather than hardcoded in the binary. It is stored in the hardcoded path <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">%APPDATA%\Microsoft\def\Gfr45.cfg</span> and the data is decrypted with a private 2048-bit RSA key (see Figure 21) using the function <a href="https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/windows/win32/api/bcrypt/nf-bcrypt-bcryptimportkeypair"><em>BCryptImportKeyPair</em></a> and <a href="https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/windows/win32/api/bcrypt/nf-bcrypt-bcryptdecrypt"><em>BCryptDecrypt</em></a>.</p>
<figure class="image"><img title="Figure 21. Hardcoded private RSA key" src="https://web-assets.esetstatic.com/wls/2023/2023-8/figure-21.png" alt="Figure 21. Hardcoded private RSA key" width="" height="" />
<figcaption>
<p class="MsoCaption"><em><a name="_Ref115448123"></a><span lang="EN-US">Figure </span><!-- [if supportFields]><span
style='mso-bookmark:_Ref115448123'></span><span style='mso-element:field-begin'></span><span
style='mso-bookmark:_Ref115448123'><span lang=EN-US><span
style='mso-spacerun:yes'> </span>SEQ Figure \* ARABIC <span style='mso-element:
field-separator'></span></span></span><![endif]--><span lang="EN-US">21</span><!-- [if supportFields]><span
style='mso-bookmark:_Ref115448123'></span><span style='mso-element:field-end'></span><![endif]--></em><span lang="EN-US"><em>. Hardcoded private RSA key</em></span></p>
</figcaption>
</figure>
<p>The config is formatted in JSON, as shown in Figure 22.&nbsp;</p>
<figure class="image"><img title="Figure 22. NightClub external configuration format" src="https://web-assets.esetstatic.com/wls/2023/2023-8/figure-22.png" alt="Figure 22. NightClub external configuration format" width="" height="" />
<figcaption>
<p class="MsoCaption"><em><a name="_Ref113632736"></a><span lang="EN-US">Figure </span><!-- [if supportFields]><span
style='mso-bookmark:_Ref113632736'></span><span style='mso-element:field-begin'></span><span
style='mso-bookmark:_Ref113632736'><span lang=EN-US><span
style='mso-spacerun:yes'> </span>SEQ Figure \* ARABIC <span style='mso-element:
field-separator'></span></span></span><![endif]--><span lang="EN-US">22</span><!-- [if supportFields]><span
style='mso-bookmark:_Ref113632736'></span><span style='mso-element:field-end'></span><![endif]--></em><span lang="EN-US"><em>. NightClub external configuration format</em></span></p>
</figcaption>
</figure>
<p>The most important keys are <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">transport</span> and <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">modules</span>. The former contains information about the mailbox used for C&amp;C communications, as in the previous versions. The latter contains the list of modules.</p>
<h5>Module agent</h5>
<p>The two variants of the module agent (SHA-1: <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">DE0B38E12C0AF0FD63A67B03DD1F8C1BF7FA6128</span> and <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">E6DE72516C1D4338D7E45E028340B54DCDC7A8AC</span>) were named <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">schvost.exe</span>, which is another imitation of the <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">svchost.exe</span> filename.</p>
<p>This component is responsible for starting the modules that are specified in the configuration. They are DLLs, each with an export named <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">Start </span>or <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;"><span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">Starts</span></span>. They are stored on disk unencrypted with the <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">.ini</span> extension, but actually are DLLs<span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">.</span></p>
<h5>Modules</h5>
<p>Over the course of our investigation, we found five different modules: an audio recorder, two almost identical screenshotters, a keylogger, and a DNS backdoor. For all of them: their configuration, which is formatted in JSON, is passed as an argument to the <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">Start </span>or<span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;"> <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">Starts</span></span> function.</p>
<p>By default, the output of the plugin is written in <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">%TEMP%\tmp123.tmp</span>. This can be changed using the config field <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">file</span>. Table 3 shows the different plugins.</p>

<td style="width: 204.9pt; border: none; border-bottom: solid #BFBFBF 1.0pt; mso-border-bottom-themecolor: background1; mso-border-bottom-themeshade: 191; mso-border-top-alt: solid #BFBFBF .5pt; mso-border-top-themecolor: background1; mso-border-top-themeshade: 191; mso-border-bottom-alt: solid #BFBFBF .5pt; padding: .55pt 5.4pt .55pt 5.4pt;" width="273">
<p class="MsoNormal" style="margin: 0cm; mso-add-space: auto; mso-yfti-cnfc: 128;"><span class="Code"><span lang="EN-US" style="mso-bidi-font-family: Arial; mso-bidi-theme-font: minor-bidi; mso-fareast-language: EN-CA;">{</span></span></p>
<p class="MsoNormal" style="margin: 0cm; mso-add-space: auto; mso-yfti-cnfc: 128;"><span class="Code"><span lang="EN-US" style="mso-bidi-font-family: Arial; mso-bidi-theme-font: minor-bidi; mso-fareast-language: EN-CA;"><span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; </span>"name":"&lt;value&gt;",</span></span></p>

<p class="MsoNormal" style="margin: 0cm; mso-add-space: auto; mso-yfti-cnfc: 128;"><span class="Code"><span lang="EN-US" style="mso-bidi-font-family: Arial; mso-bidi-theme-font: minor-bidi; mso-fareast-language: EN-CA;">}</span></span></p>

<td style="width: 154.05pt; border-top: none; border-left: none; border-bottom: solid #BFBFBF 1.0pt; mso-border-bottom-themecolor: background1; mso-border-bottom-themeshade: 191; border-right: solid #BFBFBF 1.0pt; mso-border-right-themecolor: background1; mso-border-right-themeshade: 191; mso-border-top-alt: solid #BFBFBF .5pt; mso-border-top-themecolor: background1; mso-border-top-themeshade: 191; mso-border-bottom-alt: solid #BFBFBF .5pt; mso-border-right-alt: solid #BFBFBF .5pt; padding: .55pt 5.4pt .55pt 5.4pt;" width="205">
<p class="MsoNormal" style="mso-yfti-cnfc: 128;"><span lang="EN-US" style="mso-fareast-language: EN-CA;">A DNS-tunneling backdoor. </span><span class="Code"><span lang="EN-US" style="mso-bidi-font-family: Arial; mso-bidi-theme-font: minor-bidi; mso-fareast-language: EN-CA;">cc_server_address</span></span><span lang="EN-US" style="mso-fareast-language: EN-CA;"> specifies the IP address of a DNS server to which requests are sent. More details follow.</span></p>
</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table></em></p>
<p>The DNS-tunneling backdoor (<span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">ParametersParserer.dll</span>) uses a custom protocol to send and receive data from a malicious DNS server (<span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">cc_server_address</span>). Figure 23 shows that the DNS request is sent to the IP address provided in the configuration, using the <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">pExtra</span> parameter of <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">DnsQuery_A</span>.</p>
<figure class="image"><img title="Figure 23. DNS request to the C&amp;C server" src="https://web-assets.esetstatic.com/wls/2023/2023-8/2022-plugin-dns-request.png" alt="Figure 23. DNS request to the C&amp;C server" width="" height="" />
<figcaption>
<p class="MsoCaption"><em><a name="_Ref114146244"></a><span lang="EN-US">Figure </span><!-- [if supportFields]><span
style='mso-bookmark:_Ref114146244'></span><span style='mso-element:field-begin'></span><span
style='mso-bookmark:_Ref114146244'><span lang=EN-US><span
style='mso-spacerun:yes'> </span>SEQ Figure \* ARABIC <span style='mso-element:
field-separator'></span></span></span><![endif]--><span lang="EN-US">23</span><!-- [if supportFields]><span
style='mso-bookmark:_Ref114146244'></span><span style='mso-element:field-end'></span><![endif]--></em><span lang="EN-US"><em>. DNS request to the C&amp;C server</em></span></p>
</figcaption>
</figure>
<p>The plugin adds the data to exfiltrate as part of the subdomain name of the domain that is used in the DNS request (<span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">pszName</span> above). The domain is always <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">11.1.1.cid</span> and the data is contained in the subdomain. It uses the following format, where <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">x</span> is the letter, not some variable:</p>
<p><span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">x + &lt;modified base64(buffer)&gt; + x.11.1.1.cid</span></p>
<p>For example, the first DNS request the plugin sends is <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">xZW1wdHkx.11.1.1.cid</span>, where <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">ZW1wdHk</span> decodes to <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">empty</span>.&nbsp;</p>
<p>Note that the base64 function is not standard. It removes the <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">=</span>, if any, from the result of the base64 encoding, and also replaces <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">/ </span>characters with <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">-s</span> and <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">+</span> characters with <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">-p</span>. This is to create valid subdomains, because standard base64 encoding output can include <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">+<span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">, /</span> </span>and<span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;"> <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">=</span> </span>characters, all of which are invalid in domain names and could be detected in network traffic.</p>
<p>Then, the plugin reads the result that should be one or many TXT DNS records, since the flag <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">DNS_TYPE_TEXT</span> is passed to <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">DnsQuery_A</span>. Microsoft names the <a href="https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/windows/win32/api/windns/ns-windns-dns_txt_dataa"><em>underlying structure DNS_TXT_DATAA</em></a>. It contains an array of strings, which are concatenated to compute the output buffer.</p>
<figure class="image"><img title="Figure 24. The plugin reads the TXT record" src="https://web-assets.esetstatic.com/wls/2023/2023-8/2022-dns-data-txt-1.png" alt="Figure 24. The plugin reads the TXT record" width="" height="" />
<figcaption>
<p class="MsoCaption"><em><span lang="EN-US">Figure </span><!-- [if supportFields]><span
lang=EN-US><span style='mso-element:field-begin'></span><span
style='mso-spacerun:yes'> </span>SEQ Figure \* ARABIC <span style='mso-element:
field-separator'></span></span><![endif]--><span lang="EN-US">24</span><!-- [if supportFields]><span
lang=EN-US><span style='mso-element:field-end'></span></span><![endif]--></em><span lang="EN-US"><em>. The plugin reads the TXT record</em></span></p>
</figcaption>
</figure>
<p>The expected format of the reply is:</p>
<p><span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">x + &lt;argument encoded with modified base64&gt; + x.&lt;cmd_id&gt;.&lt;unknown integer&gt;.1.&lt;cmd_name&gt;</span></p>
<p>This is similar to the format of the requests. The <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">&lt;argument encoded with modified base64&gt;</span> also uses the custom base64 encoding without <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">=</span> and with <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">-p</span> for <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">+</span> and <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">-s</span> for <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">/</span>. <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">&lt;cmd_name&gt;</span> is an arbitrary string that is not used by the backdoor; it&rsquo;s likely used by the operators to keep track of the different commands. <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">&lt;cmd_id&gt;</span> is an integer that corresponds to a command in the backdoor <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">switch</span> statement.&nbsp;</p>
<p>For example, if the operators wanted to execute <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">calc.exe</span>, the DNS C&amp;C server would send the reply <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">xYzpcd2luZG93c1xzeXN0ZW0zMlxjYWxjLmV4ZQx.27.2.1.calc</span>, where <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">Yzpcd2luZG93c1xzeXN0ZW0zMlxjYWxjLmV4ZQ</span> decodes to <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">c:\windows\system32\calc.exe</span> and <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">27</span> is the command ID to create a new process. All commands supported by this backdoor are detailed in Table 4.</p>
<p style="text-align: center;"><em>Table 4. Commands implemented by the DNS backdoor</em></p>
<p style="text-align: left;"><em><table class="MsoTable15Grid1Light" style="border-collapse: collapse; border: none; mso-border-alt: solid #BFBFBF .5pt; mso-border-themecolor: background1; mso-border-themeshade: 191; mso-yfti-tbllook: 1056; mso-padding-alt: .55pt 5.4pt .55pt 5.4pt;" border="1" cellspacing="0" cellpadding="0">

</table></em></p>
<p>The result of the commands is exfiltrated back to the attacker using DNS requests, as detailed above. The only difference is that <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">11</span> is replaced by <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">12</span> in the domain name, as shown in this example: <span style="font-family: 'courier new', courier, monospace;">xdGltZW91dAx.12.1.1.cid</span>. In this case, the plugin sent the message timeout to the C&amp;C server.</p>
<h2>Conclusion</h2>
<p>MoustachedBouncer is a skilled threat actor targeting foreign diplomats in Belarus. It uses quite advanced techniques for C&amp;C communications including network interception at the ISP level for the Disco implant, emails for the NightClub implant, and DNS in one of the NightClub plugins.</p>
<p>The main takeaway is that organizations in foreign countries where the internet cannot be trusted should use an end-to-end encrypted VPN tunnel to a trusted location for all their internet traffic in order to circumvent any network inspection devices.</p>

<p class="MsoNormal" style="mso-yfti-cnfc: 68;"><strong><span lang="EN-US" style="mso-fareast-language: EN-CA;">Impact</span></strong></p>
</td>
<td style="width: 3.0cm; border: none; border-bottom: solid #BFBFBF 1.0pt; mso-border-bottom-themecolor: background1; mso-border-bottom-themeshade: 191; mso-border-top-alt: solid #A6A6A6 .5pt; mso-border-top-themecolor: background2; mso-border-top-themeshade: 166; mso-border-bottom-alt: solid #BFBFBF .5pt; padding: .55pt 5.4pt .55pt 5.4pt; height: 22.7pt;" width="113">
<p class="MsoNormal" style="mso-yfti-cnfc: 64;"><span lang="EN-US" style="mso-fareast-language: EN-CA;"><a href="https://attack.mitre.org/versions/v13/techniques/T1565/002"><em style="mso-bidi-font-style: normal;">T1565.002</em></a></span></p>
</td>
<td style="width: 4.0cm; border: none; border-bottom: solid #BFBFBF 1.0pt; mso-border-bottom-themecolor: background1; mso-border-bottom-themeshade: 191; mso-border-top-alt: solid #A6A6A6 .5pt; mso-border-top-themecolor: background2; mso-border-top-themeshade: 166; mso-border-bottom-alt: solid #BFBFBF .5pt; padding: .55pt 5.4pt .55pt 5.4pt; height: 22.7pt;" width="151">
<p class="MsoNormal" style="mso-yfti-cnfc: 64;"><span lang="EN-US" style="mso-fareast-language: EN-CA;">Data Manipulation: Transmitted Data Manipulation</span></p>
</td>
<td style="width: 7.0cm; border-top: none; border-left: none; border-bottom: solid #BFBFBF 1.0pt; mso-border-bottom-themecolor: background1; mso-border-bottom-themeshade: 191; border-right: solid #BFBFBF 1.0pt; mso-border-right-themecolor: background1; mso-border-right-themeshade: 191; mso-border-top-alt: solid #A6A6A6 .5pt; mso-border-top-themecolor: background2; mso-border-top-themeshade: 166; mso-border-bottom-alt: solid #BFBFBF .5pt; mso-border-right-alt: solid #BFBFBF .5pt; padding: .55pt 5.4pt .55pt 5.4pt; height: 22.7pt;" width="265">
<p class="MsoNormal" style="mso-yfti-cnfc: 64;"><span lang="EN-US" style="mso-fareast-language: EN-CA;">MoustachedBouncer has modified the HTTP traffic from specific IP addresses at the ISP level in order to redirect its targets to a fake Windows Update page.</span></p>
</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table></p>
